0062183591|5.0|\\"If we're honest with ourselves, we know we're not honest with ourselves\\" - Kevin Nenstiel<br /><br />Dan Ariely, in his book The (Honest) Truth About Dishonesty (How We Lie to Everyone--Especially Ourselves), shows how most of think ourselves as honest, but, in fact, we all cheat. Unethical behaviour is pervasive and none of us are immune. This pragmatic book is an outstanding example of how to apply social science in general and behavioural economics in particular, to daily life and invent new approaches to improve our results.<br /><br />Dan Ariely, the founder and director of the Center for Advanced Hindsight, is the authority about irrational behaviour and he is ideally positioned to write a book about the forces that make us cheat and about the best ways to control them. A brilliant behavioural economist, Ariely also shows how hard it is to see which forces are irrelevant and which are at work.<br /><br />First, contrary to popular belief, dishonesty is not a result of rational cost-benefit analysis. Even if we kick the doors wide open and send all the guards home, few people would plunder the entire treasury but most people will help themselves to some of it.<br /><br />Next, Ariely shows that dishonesty is pervasive, and, most importantly, we do not see it in ourselves.<br /><br />Third, Ariely shows that cheating is result of a balancing act between two forces, namely, the desire to benefit and the desire to think of ourselves as essentially good. So, we cheat, regardless of consequences and as long as we can still rationalize our cheating as essentially good behaviour. That's, for instance, how we miss the omnipresent conflicts of interest.<br /><br />Fourth, Ariely shows how dishonesty spreads virally. When we see people who essentially resemble us get away with dishonesty, we are more likely to cheat ourselves. On the other hand, if we see others resist dishonesty, our likelihood of virtuous behavior increases. Irrationally, we seek role models.<br /><br />Having made these fundamental discoveries, Ariely draws recommendations to curb dishonesty and then gives examples of their application in practice:<br /><br />1. Both the traditional liberal and the conservative solutions to curb dishonesty are ineffective. Rely neither on regulations nor on penalties to prevent dishonesty, because people ignore cost-benefit calculations.<br />2. Create conditions where dishonesty would become far more difficult to self-justify because it requires a more deliberate, more intentional activity,<br />3. Establish social norm of honesty, find and celebrate role models or honesty,<br />4. Create resetting events, periodic endpoints for dishonesty and opportunities for new beginnings, e.g., remind about moral code: even atheists cheat less after swearing on a Bible.<br /><br />Read this book and observe how it changes the way you think about your relationships and your work./>Having made these fundamental discoveries, Ariely draws recommendations to curb dishonesty and then gives examples of their application in practice:<br /><br />1. 0871404133|5.0|Social Conquest at once presents a groundbreaking scientific analysis of evolution and a strangely enigmatic rumination on our purpose and future on Earth. What you will discover in the text itself and also in the firestorm of reviews (Richard Dawkins in Prospect and in New York Review of Books to name a few) is that contemporary biologists are relegated to living in the shadow of Darwin, who we will still see as the greatest naturalist of the 20th and presumably the 21st centuries. In comparison to what Darwin eloquently and bravely exposed, Social Conquest and other works of Wilson (Dawkins as well for that matter) do not match the standards. Much of the battle between these two great scientific minds (Wilson and Dawkins) could be interpreted as their struggle to claim leadership of bio-evolutionary theory post-Darwin.<br /><br />I will briefly describe what I found to be the most profound ideas in Social Conquest, beginning with his theory of multi-level selection. Other reviewers have commented thoroughly on the extent to which ant behavior is analyzed in this current work. To say that it is a book about Ants, however, is sorely off track. Wilson shares his intimate knowledge of their behavior as an example of Group Selection, the real subject of the fiery scientific debate. Another advantage of using ants as an example is that their social structure is highly complex relative to almost any other species on the planet. Their rich organizational structure provides ample opportunities to anthropormorhpize!  However, to draw parallels from ant society to our own is not to say that humans evolved like ants, but to expose the forces that perhaps drove both species to invest more energy in a society rather than in individual survival.<br /><br />Secondly, we see Wilson's philosophical side emerge in his last hundred or so pages of the text. The discussions are a bit narrow and leave room to be explored further, but I do not see this as a negative. Remaining within hisbounds as a scientist, he provides many valuable insights into the genealogy of religion and the value of morality and honor. Raising mass consciousness to this level would surely be an superlative achievement. However, as Wilson knows all to well, humans want to preserve their status when they have it good, and will ignore their \\"group\\" instincts often to the peril of themselves and anyone else who they drag down. We are at a critical point in history. Mainstream \\"know how\\" and current attempts at \\"fixing the system\\" are ultimately wildly insufficient. What Wilson conveys is that we need fundamental change in order to ultimately embrace our existence as organisms. B007I2BZIE|5.0|Best album since Moving Pictures - I've been a Rush fan since I was 10 with the 1976 release of 2112. I have to say that this album is my favorite since that seminal album in 1981 (Moving Pictures) and really the only one since that time that I listen to the entire album and honestly love every single track. It is a work of art. If you had told me this was some long lost piece originally recorded in that period of '75-82 I would not be surprised. But it is all new original material and I think 20 years from now people will be talking about this album as a classic too. It is hard to believe they created something so original, fresh and at the same time classic and timeless 30+ years after that epic album. But here it is. I was really moved by this album and each track deserves some mention:<br /><br />Caravan - \\"I can't stop thinking big\\"<br />Best opening track since Tom Sawyer, like that song it is a descriptive narrative of the world we are in lit by fire and driven by steamliners, paints a picture of our young hero with big dreams. Great riff. Drives forward relentlessly like the road it describes.<br /><br />BU2B -\\"I was brought up to believe...\\"<br />Title is an acronym for the line bove.....and the people in this world are taught to blindly follow and do as they are told. Starts slow with an intro and then rocks onward driving the narrative.<br /><br />Clockwork Angels - \\"You promise every treasure, to the foolish and the wise, Goddesses of mystery, spirits in disguise\\"<br />Best song on the album, Neil Peart at his best, musical and melodic interludes interspersed with a driving bass and drumline chorus, this will be a concert favorite. Gives a vivid picture of soaring over grandiose cities in a distant land.<br /><br />The Anarchist - \\"What I know, I've never shown; what I feel, I've always known\\"<br />The only less than stellar track on the album, still enjoyable though, a straightforward rocker. Our hero joins the circus to get away, but is blamed for a criminal act and is cast out himself.<br /><br />Halo Effect - \\"Fool that I was, A goddess, with wings on her heels, All my illusions projected on her,  The ideal, that I wanted to see\\"<br />The most emotional and deep Rush song in recent memory. A sad song but very memorable, a great rock ballad.  He idealizes a beautiful goddess he sees and is crushed by a love lost and a cruel world.<br /><br />Seven Cities of Gold - \\"a man can lose himself in a country like this...\\"<br />Great opening bass line, screeching guitars and drums giving it rhythm leading into a soaring rocker. He is thinking about the past failures and the dreams he once had.<br /><br />The Wreckers -  \\"All I know is that sometimes you have to be wary\\"<br />A cautionary tale with a catchy riff, 2nd best song on the album, like no Rush song I can ever remember, at the same time it feels familiar but is totally unique. Beautiful imagery throughout, you can feel the ocean and salty air as our hero is aboard a ship in a storm facing possible doom.<br /><br />Headlong Flight - \\"I learned to fight, I learned to love, I learned to feel, How I wish that I could live it all again\\"<br />A reeling rocker with a real sense of urgency, sure to be a concert favorite with a driving beat and soaring vocals and guitar riffs. It's the end of the journey for the hero who has regrets and must leave behind youthful idealism for the realities of the world he is in.<br /><br />BU2B2 - \\"Life goes from bad to worse. I still choose to live\\"<br />An interlude at best, leading to the closing tracks...his optimism is gone, but he keeps going forward...<br /><br />Wish Them Well - \\"All that you can do is wish them well, Even though you're going through hell\\"<br />A catchy rocker, modern in feel, this could easily have been a Foo Fighters song. He has learned not to dwell on the negative or let it affect you, just move on...<br /><br />The Garden - \\"It is what it is\\"<br />A fitting closing number in which you step back and realize its ok to dream, time is not the enemy because it is what you do with the time you have that matters. Sometimes the control's state and visible state get reversed (it will be on when shown as off, and off when shown as on).<br />* The camera is no longer discoverable from the app. I don't know if this is a problem with the app or the camera. I can still use the web view but the app is now useless.<br />* The manufacturer has never provided a firmware update for the camera, so still no IPv6 support.<br /><br />If you have the ability, I highly recommend getting a Raspberry Pi with a camera connector board. It's cheaper, works better and lasts longer. If that's not your thing, I still discourage buying this camera. Look for a better brand, hopefully one that will last longer.

B00000613T|2.0|Mahler's ninth symphony is one of my favorite pieces of music. I have listened to many recordings, and this is not one that I find very likable. It's a shame, because the first, second, and third movements are all gripping and lush, but the Adagio (final movement) made me very disappointed. Make note, this review is focusing exclusively on the fourth movement.<br /><br />I like the movement to be taken very slowly, around 45 bpm, while Boulez's interpretation seems to be closer to or in excess of 55. You may say, \\"well, 10 bpm isn't much of a difference!\\" But, let me assure you, the difference is immediately noticeable. At the tempo it's taken, I feel the phrases don't speak very well, and the performance is significantly less dramatic, intense, and breathtaking than it could have been.<br /><br />What I actually found amusing, is during the glorious horn solo (you know the one), the principle horn player actually plays at about 45, and the orchestra accommodates as if planned (which it very well could be), but then the moment it is finished, the tempo jumps back up to 55-ish. I don't know if the musician was intentionally slowing the tempo (unlikely), or whether it was supposed to be that way, but it sounds bizarre to me.<br /><br />I do acknowledge, however, that because the reason for me finding the performance dissatisfying is purely a tempo issue, it is possible you may like it. Even though the first Terminator had aged quite a bit in the seven years between films, T2's sharp, daring script constantly cites events, characters and situations from its timeline like a religious zealot thumping the bible. CGI was still in its infancy in the early '90s, but James Cameron's knack for its timely, appropriate application is already at veteran levels in T2. As a direct result, those infamous scenes with a transmorphing T-1000 remain striking and impressive, where other effects of the period now seem clunky and unrealistic. Not only is it one of the best sequels ever, it's among the very best of the batch in multiple genres, and Arnold has never been more at-home in a character. That said, it's about slavery, so much as we'd like to re-imagine history I think the author actually resisted temptation and told a rich, realistic story and she should be commended for that. If anything, it probably IS rather optimistic compared to reality.<br /><br />The novel alternates between two narrators, one white and one black, both caught in an 'in between' status between traditional white and black roles in the South.<br /><br />The first is Lavinia, a white orphaned Irish indentured servant who comes to work for 'The Captain' after her parents die on the trans-Atlantic voyage. She is sent to live with some higher status slaves (Mamma Mae and Papa George and their children, who have both earned special status through their service to the family), and Belle in the kitchen house. Lavinia grows to love them, and they her, and as she grows into a beauty and begins to also build closer relationships with the plantation owning family, things get complicated. Lavinia's fatal flaw is her growing mistrust of her slave family the more she is influenced by the white owners.<br /><br />The second narrator is Belle, who is actually the illegitimate daughter of the Captain. The Captain cares about Belle and gives her special status and privilege among the slaves. He promises to give her freedom papers before retiring. The Captain never tells his wife the truth about who Belle is, however, and she is driven to madness in her jealousy, assuming Belle is his lover. This mis-belief has terrible repercussions for every single member of the family. Belle's fatal flaw is her trust that the Captain will always protect her.<br /><br />Although Belle is a blood relation to the Captain, her status dwindles throughout the book. Alternatively, Lavinia is able to transcend her lowly beginnings simply by virtue of being white and beautiful. Neither can ever really seize their opportunities for happiness, though, because they are unwilling to leave those they love who are enslaved. You'll grow attached to most of the characters, and feel their emotions (fear, anticipation, heartbreak, hope) fully. Anyway ...on with the review.<br /><br />The story starts with our protagonist, Vaelin Al Sorna - known as a Brother of the 6th Order, the Sword of the Realm, Young Hawk, Darkblade, Beral Shak Ur, and Hope Killer, being led as a prisoner by Lord Verniers Alshe Someren, Imperial Chronicler, First of the Learned and honored servant to the Empire, to face a judgment by a country and people whom his Battle Lord father had almost decimated when he was but a small child. Already prejudiced against him for being the son of their most hated rival, Vaelin is also the killer of their beloved Hope, the successor to the Emperor's throne in their latest skirmish with King Janus and the Realm. As they travel across the sea to face a trial by conquest, Vaelin tells this chronicler the story of his life and how the most hated of villains he became.<br /><br />This prologue pulled me right into the story and piqued my interest to the point I just had to read on. That's one of the formula's I love best - have a despicable state of being and then go back and show me how one achieved such a state. It is the basic fundamental aspect of humanity - to understand how someone reaches a certain state in their life, either deplorable or exceptional, so we can better understand and see the path to that destruction or success and apply the principles to our own lives.<br /><br />The real story begins with Vaelin as a mere child dealing the death of his mother, along with losing that innocent childhood, as his Battle Lord father abandons him to the Brothers of the Sixth Order, a sect of the Faith that focuses primarily on the art of war. It is his time with the Sixth Order that we fall in love with Vaelin, and his other brothers, as they are transformed from weak, innocent boys, into a close-nit team of merciless assassins. We are carried right along with Vaelin as he develops his skills, face his trials, and nurtures the hate for the father that abandoned him. You can't help but root for him and his Order brothers as they transform from the fearful into the greatly feared. Anthony Ryan does an excellent job of sewing the reader so perfectly secure into all the emotion and action that has you completely solidified and connected with the protagonist.<br /><br />What is a great adventure without a little love to soften the hardness, - bring a little light into the darkness, than to have a splash of romance? I love the way the author didn't douse the reader in sappy syrup, but sprinkled just enough to allow the sweetness to be savored on top of the spice. My mind went into three different directions on this aspect of the story, but I'm very pleased with how it turned it out. I loved the twists and turns the story took, but can't express how much I loved how the author kept the priorities in line. This is usually where a writer plunges off an unseen cliff, especially with epic fantasies - they allow the romance to overcome and dominate the story, instead of balancing it. It's hard to do, because it's so easy to get carried away with emotion, but Anthony Ryan handled it very well. At least, I'm well pleased. I hate seeing a great super strong character lose all his strength and integrity for a woman. As a woman, I want a strong-willed man; not a limp-wristed, love sick puppy. A valiant warrior, who has strength to lead and fight, must also have strength of mind, yet time and time again you find these oxymorons posed as heroes, men who can stand against all the world, but can't say no to a woman??? Okay, time to moveaway from this particular rant and on with the review.<br /><br />There is another twist I absolutely LOVED in this story, but I don't want to say too much about it because it will give it away. Let's just say - I didn't see it coming, and it broke my heart - literally. THAT is hard to do. I've read and written so many stories that I'm rarely surprised, but in this - I was absolutely blown away. Not only was the foundation and story thread brilliantly laid, but exceptionally executed. Well done, Anthony! If I had any criticism of this entire story, it would be this: Put it through one more round of edits (especially where commas are concerned) and get this traditionally published so it can receive greater exposure.<br /><br />I don't know Mr. Ryan, but I've come to admire his work, just as I know you will too if you dare to delve into the adventure of Raven's Song.<br /><br />Till next time,<br />~ Author T.L. 1400031001|5.0|William Boyd's novel is the autobiography of Logan Mountstuart, son of a British father and Uruguayan mother, who begins life seemingly under the most auspicious of stars. From a prosperous family, he spends his early years in South America and then in Birmingham, attending a fine public school and then Oxford and producing his first published work in his twenties.  He marries an Earl's daughter and has a son, and it all seems so effortless and pleasant.  But shadows arise.  He falls out of love with his wife and with another woman. His writing career bogs down into a series of jobs at journals which eventually go out of business. When he does write a book it's well reviewed but sells poorly.  Things revive a bit during World War II, when he is recruited by Ian Fleming to work for British Intelligence, but this leads to his capture and imprisonment.  When he returns to England after the war it is only to be faced with more tragedy. Logan goes on writing sporadically, develops a new career as an art dealer in New York, falls in love with another woman and marries her, and for a time all seems well.  Eventually troubles reemerge and Logan gradually but steadily goes down hill, growing poorer and poorer as the years go by.  When he finally dies at the age of 85 he is barely remembered and turns out to have destroyed his final novel before it was finished.<br /><br />This bare synopsis sounds mostly depressing, but there are many engaging and sometimes lighthearted moments in this tale. Logan meets and has dealings with a large number of famous (sometimes notorious) people, including the Duke and Duchess of Windsor. Facilitating the conceit that this is a real autobiography of an actual person are the footnotes and editorial inserts that identify the famous people with whom Logan is said to come into contact.  Logan's story in many ways parallels the story of Britain in the twentieth century, or at least of the type of British society into which he was born and moved during his early life.  Any Human Heart is a long read but an interesting one, and while the reader may justly feel that Logan was his own worst enemy and that he brought many of his troubles on himself, nevertheless he'll come away with a liking for the man.ring his early life. B0071XO8RA|5.0|Fantastic books.  Wonderfully executed, good clean narratives, and they don't drag at any point.  Even the twists at the end of each don't feel contrived, they simply break open an entire new path in the books' Universe to explore.  These were incredibly enjoyable and I would wholeheartedly recommend them to anyone.  I would also say that two things struck me about these books.<br /><br />First, thank god for Ebooks and self-publishing.  It is so refreshing to have cleared the path between the consumer and supplier when it comes to media.  Mr. Howey can charge six dollars for the entire series, the majority of it coming back to him essentially, because the parasitic publishing companies are cut out.  Mr. Howey should be commended for not only writing a truly great work of science fiction, but for also helping to sound the death knell of the modern publishing company.<br /><br />When it comes to media, there is nothing worse than the middleman.  So rarely is anything added by a publisher, and if rough edges exist then so be it, they add to the experience in the aggregate as different authors take chances that they wouldn't normally be allowed to take.  An editor with an author's best interests at heart is becoming a rarer and rarer commodity within today's publishing companies.  I have witnessed firsthand what is occurring at the larger publishing companies, and as revenues shrink authors and their art are sacrificed at the altar for the short term bottomline.<br /><br />The modern bloated publishing companies (with the concentration on marketing, supply, distribution, shelf space in the bookstore) are dead.  But they just don't know it yet or won't accept it.  Within the next ten years they will go the way of Blockbuster and all corporations like it.  Amazon cut their knees off and they never realized it.  And thank god.  Please continue what you are doing Mr. Howey, and to all other authors out there like you.  Allow yourself to profit from yourself.  It is your art, your product and your effort.  Well done.<br /><br />My second point is that I have truly never read a series that is begging more to be made into a television series.  HBO and AMC are the only two networks that come to mind with the resources and audacity to actually turn these stories into something to watch.  But honestly, the characters, the visuals, everything about these stories make me want to see them done in series format.  Not a single movie, that would be terrible, too crammed in and you would lose the atmosphere, the characters, the world that they live in.  A television series would be perfect for this.  Please, allow someone with half a brain to pick this up and make it something incredible.  And for god's sake don't put it on any of the three major networks.  Their programming is the definition of Lowest Common Denominator and everything that is wrong with appealing to the widest possible audience.<br /><br />Anyway, like I said, wonderful, fantastically done and anyone who enjoys science fiction should buy these books.

B005LAIHXQ|4.0|&lt;strong>Prometheus&lt;/strong> (Ridley Scott, 2012)<br /><br />You know what? If &lt;em>Prometheus&lt;/em> had been directed by anyone other than Ridley Scott, or if it hadn't been marketed as an &lt;em>Alien&lt;/em> prequel--I am working on the hypothesis that it originally was not, and the &lt;em>Alien&lt;/em> elements were retrofitted either at the insistence of the studio or when Scott and Spaits/Lindelof realized how much there was in common there--we wouldn't be having any of the discussions we are currently having about this movie. We'd probably be pretty united in hailing it as one of the more inventive (if derivative), and certainly one of the most technically accomplished, science fiction movies in recent memory, probably the best sci-fi movie since &lt;em>Cube&lt;/em> almost fifteen years ago. From a technical standpoint, &lt;em>Prometheus&lt;/em> is unassailable; the acting is pitch-perfect, the direction is exactly what one would expect form the guy who brought you &lt;em>Gladiator&lt;/em> and &lt;em>Bladerunner&lt;/em>, every member of the crew brought his or her A game, the 3D is hands-down the best I've seen since &lt;em>Cave of Forgotten Dreams&lt;/em> (and is used much in the same way, to my delight). In short, this is the type of movie for which the word \\"sumptuous\\" was coined. Forget all that Merchant-Ivory boondoggle. (After all, what are sci-fi films if not period dramas for periods that haven't happened yet?) It is, in short, an impressive achievement by any empirical yardstick one would choose to measure it with.<br /><br />The problem is, of course, that it &lt;em>was&lt;/em> directed by Ridley Scott, and even if you hadn't caught all the in-jokes and references in the script before you hit the final sequence, well, when you get to that point there's no denying this is an &lt;em>Alien&lt;/em> movie. (And in a movie that seemed awfully retrofitted, that final sequence was the most garishly tacked-on bit. But I am getting ahead of myself.) Now, while I am a pretty darned big fan of the Alien series, or at least the first three movies (I still to this day have not gotten round to seeing &lt;em>Alien Resurrection&lt;/em>, despite pretty much everyone I know telling me how much better it is than &lt;em>Alien&lt;supr>3&lt;/supr>&lt;/em>, a movie I actually quite liked), and even the one guy in the room who actually enjoyed &lt;em>Alien vs. Predator&lt;/em> for the stupid fun fanboy movie it was, I'm still a casual fan of the series as opposed to a true &lt;em>otaku&lt;/em>. You kind of have to be, in order to enjoy the big stuff-blows-up stupidity of &lt;em>Alien vs. Predator&lt;/em> in any way.<br /><br />There's also another layer to this, and one that most people don't seem to have thought about, but that's come up a few times in my discussions on the movie since I saw it Friday night (I'm writing this on the following Monday afternoon); there's a meta layer that was absent from the other films. And perhaps I'm overstepping the bounds here, but it seems to me that Scott and co., and specifically Avy Kaufman, who cast Noomi Rapace as a character named Elizabeth (and in at least one place during an action scene, an actor slips up and calls her character Lisbet), pretty much invited this meta layer into the film. And it casts an interesting light on what has become one of the movie's big controversies. But again, I'm getting ahead of myself.<br /><br />NOTE: There is one thing revealed in the synopsis that I can't believe is a spoiler, though there is a scene in the film that leads me to believe it was meant that way. If you are especially spoiler-sensitive, you may want to skip over the synopsis.<br /><br />Plot: two sceintists, Elizabeth Shaw (Rapace, rocketed to fame through playing Lisbeth Salander in the Millennium trilogy) and Charlie Holloway (&lt;em>Devil&lt;/em>'s Logan Marshall-Green), uncover what would seem to be a star map in the cave paintings and stone carvings of half a dozen, maybe more,ancient civilizations. Thanks to new-and-improved 2089 technology, they pinpoint the exact star system those paintings depict.<br /><br />[Problem #1: 35,000+ years later, the positions of some of those stars will have changed; after a tenth of that, some of the Greek constellations have lost their original shapes to modern eyes.]<br /><br />So Weyland Technologies, headed up by the very, very old Peter Weyland (&lt;em>The Adventures of Priscilla&lt;/em>'s Guy Pearce, and I'd be tempted to ask him which film had the tougher make-up job) and his ice-queen daughter Meredith Vickers (&lt;em>Monster&lt;/em>'s Charlize Theron), send a ship out to this star system, which happens to contain one very earth-like moon revolving a Saturn-like planet. The ship contains seventeen crew members, including various scientists, navigators, Vickers as the Weyland Technologies representative, and an android named David (&lt;em>Shame&lt;/em>'s Michael Fassbender) who learns human mannerisms from watching old movies obsessively, but, as a hologram of Weyland reminds us during the mission briefing, has no soul. Holloway and Shaw believe this planet to be the home of a race they call the Engineers--advanced beings who created the human race. When they get to the planet they do indeed find evidence that something other than nature has been there (\\"God does not create in straight lines,\\" as Holloway tells us), but of course, what they are seeking and what they find are two entirely different things...<br /><br />As regards the meta I spoke of earlier, and its particular application to &lt;em>Prometheus&lt;/em>: Michael Fassbender garnered a great deal of critical acclaim for his role in &lt;em>Shame&lt;/em>, playing a sex addict. An interesting choice to play the role of an asexual robot, especially when so many of your audience members leave asking themselves (or the people they were with) \\"was David gay?\\". It's a frankly ridiculous question to even be asking, and while half of me doubts that 90%+ of the people involved in the debate are even aware &lt;em>Shame&lt;/em> exists, it still feels like that particular casting choice forced the issue, rather like not changing the name of Noomi Rapace's character. And I've been mulling on this for days without being able to come up with another motive than \\"provide non-controversy to get butts in seats.\\" Which seems to be working, but it feels a little disingenuous.<br /><br />But you know what else? I'm going to ask you to forget all that. I'm also going to ask you to forget about all those plot holes that aren't really plot holes (if you're watching the movie on DVD, pause it when they reveal the designation of the planet, then go back and watch the first &lt;em>Alien&lt;/em>; they're two different, albeit somehow related, planets), forget about the anachronisms that make this ship so much technologically cooler (despite predating it) than the Nostromo, forget this movie is in any way related to the Alien franchise, which it actually &lt;em>is&lt;/em> possible to do until you get to that last sequence that drove me up the wall. Instead, focus on the other obvious source material from which they drew when writing this script: &lt;em>2001&lt;/em>. All the stuff about exploration and the dawn of man and all that. That, to me, is where the core of this film lay: in the sense of wonder at the discoveries they were making. &lt;em>That&lt;/em> is what this movie was about, and in that, it succeeded as well as it could given the script tinkering and retrofitting and all the other silliness. All of which means this is not a perfect movie by any means, but it is a lovely one, and worth seeing. *** ¬Ωpaintings depict.<br /><br />[Problem #1: 35,000+ years later, the positions of some of those stars will have changed; after a tenth of that, some of the Greek constellations have lost their original shapes to modern eyes.]<br /><br />So Weyland Technologies, headed up by the very, very old Peter Weyland (<em>The Adventures of Priscilla</em>'s Guy Pearce, and I'd be tempted to ask him which film had the tougher make-up job) and his ice-queen daughter Meredith Vickers (<em>Monster</em>'s Charlize Theron), send a ship out to this star system, which happens to contain one very earth-like moon revolving a Saturn-like planet. Gosling has been treading the same basic ground as Joseph Gordon-Levitt, and doing it just as well, for the same time period, but we look at him and think \\"hey, it's that nice, clean-cut kid from &lt;em>The Notebook&lt;/em>!\\". And then he pops up in &lt;em>Drive&lt;/em> and people still manage to be shocked. (Go back and re-watch &lt;em>The Believer&lt;/em>, which is just as good as &lt;em>Drive&lt;/em>, for a little perspective.)<br /><br />Gosling plays Driver, a mechanic/stunt driver who moonlights as a freelance getaway guy for small-time crooks pulling heists. (The opening sequence of the film is a great account of this side job.) All is going well in Driver's life until Irene (&lt;em>An Education&lt;/em>'s Carey Mulligan) and her son Benicio (Kaden Leos in his screen debut), his neighbors from down the hall, enter his life. Driver starts to see what a life he has never allowed himself--a stable home, a family--looks like, and it's a powerful attraction. Problem is, Irene's husband Standard (&lt;em>Sucker Punch&lt;/em>'s Oscar Isaac) is getting out of prison. And he's in debt to some guys. And maybe needs a getaway driver...<br /><br />The plot, from a Hossein Amini script adapted from a James Sallis novel, is pretty standard stuff for &lt;em>noir&lt;/em>, where everyone's a criminal and life is incapable of ending well for anyone involved. The best parts of this film are in the way Amini writes the characters, especially the minor characters, and how Refn brings those characters to life. (It helps that most of those minor characters are played by very big names, including Ron Perlman, Bryan Cranston, Albert Brooks, Christina Hendricks, and Russ Tamblyn, among others.) Our ability to empathize with Driver, a character written to have the best poker face in the movies, is pretty impressive, and that's down to the combination of Amini's writing, Refn's directing, and Gosling's acting, all of which are impeccable here.<br /><br />The movie only really goes wrong in its incidentals. The soundtrack is &lt;em>awful&lt;/em>, saccharine and overbearing and as subtle as a sledgehammer to the face. In other words, it is everything the script is not, and it detracts from the movie in a fundamental way by being so painfully obvious. There's also a sense that, towards the end, some stuff is missing, though that sense is minor enough that I could have been reading more into it than there actually was (specifically, I got the feeling there was history between Rose and Driver/Shannon that went way beyond what we saw, but there's zero actual evidence of this save some of the things Rose says to each of them towards the end of the movie). If I'm right, well, I'd have gladly spent another half an hour in the company of these characters and the dark clouds that hang over them in order to explore that further.<br /><br />And while I've been hammering on the acting job Gosling did in this flick, I don't want to take away from Carey Mulligan's job here, which is heartbreaking. There's a great, great shot in this flick as we're looking at her through a stationary camera placed in an elevator as the door's getting ready to close. She stands stock-still and everything we need to know about not only her character, but the entire rest of the movie, is contained in the expression on her face. As much as this word is overused in American dialogue, I think it's warranted here--that shot is awesome, in the classic sense of the word. It inspires awe. I'd stick it in the modern film canon right there with the abduction scene in &lt;em>Rabbit-Proof Fence&lt;/em> or the ten-minute shot at the center of Haneke's &lt;em>Funny Games&lt;/em> or the dance at the beginning of &lt;em>Werckmeister Harmonies&lt;/em>. It's that good.<br /><br />Do I need to tell you I'm recommending this movie? See it ASAP. ****ere's a great, great shot in this flick as we're looking at her through a stationary camera placed in an elevator as the door's getting ready to close. She stands stock-still and everything we need to know about not only her character, but the entire rest of the movie, is contained in the expression on her face. 0143113100|4.0|Norman Doidge, M.D.'s The Brain that Changes Itself is a neuroscience enthusiast's Chicken Soup for (Insert Audience) equivalent. The book includes heartwarming success stories of neurological disabled victims, the scientific pursuits of neuroscience pioneers, and scientific theory. The highlights of this book are putting scientific principles (i.e. neuroplasticity, mapping, neuronal regeneration) in layman's terms by using examples of people who have been studied and seen to undergo these concepts. In a case study, a woman had rewired her half brain to act as a whole one. However, the explanations of neuroscientific concepts are brief and cannot substitute learning the material textbook. Also, the accounts are misleading in which all severe brain damaged people will achieve extremely successful neuroplasticity and normal function. As another highlight, the personal accounts magically give scientists a personality beyond the lab bench. Dr. Doidge poignantly writes on the motivations of each individual scientist, their process of reasoning, and how they established the frontiers of neuroscience. To an established researcher, many of the essential lab techniques are missing from the text, and only general overviews are given for research that spans at least 20 years. Although the book reads like a Freakanomics where multiple cases are presented, the lack of evident plot and unrelated accounts proves to be overdone by the end of the 427-page book. Thus, due to the entertainment and simple, easy-to-read style for the average person on a complex subject balanced with lengthiness and generality, this book is reviewed at 4 out of 5 stars.

Most of their biggest hits had Dennis DeYoung as lead vocalist (Come Sail Away, Mr. Roboto, Don't Let It End, Best of Times) but PIECES OF EIGHT had guitarist Tommy Shaw voicing its two hit singles--RENEGADE and BLUE COLLAR MAN.<br />Styx also bebefitted from its other musicians, especially vocalistguitarist James Young.  PIECES OF EIGHT is Styx at an apex in their career---they kept their die-hard fans but grabbed a new, wider audience in the traditional pop/rock arena.  My favorite tracks:  RENEGADE, a frenetic rocker with an awesome a cappella opening and blistering guitar work; the appropriately theatrical bombast of LORDS OF THE RING; DeYoung's QUEEN OF SPADES and PIECES OF EIGHT, which open with lyric balladry and then explode into seething rockers. And the sumptuously elegant AKU AKU, a remarkable rock instrumental penned by Mr. Shaw.<br />PIECES OF EIGHT is an important 70s album, ushering in a beacon for other prog/rock bands to become more accessible to the mainstream musical audience without sacrificing its inherent musical direction.

B007FZ7QZA|5.0|Now I'll make no bones about my love of studio oriented,live band soul,funk and R&B from the 70's and early 80's in particular. However music has moved on and it's now various hybrids of hip-hop that is more influential on that genre. Because I shut myself off from changes in the music that occurred during the 1990's I only have myself to blame for finding myself in a musical world I am still finding baffling. True there is a lot of pop music out there lacking almost in any creative ideas. And it's been only recently,for reasons I am not sure about that I began to even take a second notice at the music of Chris Brown. He's been around for more than half a decade. And as stated in my previous review of his album [[ASIN:B000T988DI Exclusive]],I didn't think much of his character. But even with the egoist sound of this introduction,it's the music that interested me. And even with all the whoopdy doo in the press about him I wanted to hear for myself what he was all about. Well I liked the album I just reviewed by him well enough. But this is something quite different.<br /><br />  Electropop has largely bought bubblegum beyond the dark ages. But somehow Chris does something with it here that actually advances the music forward.\\"Turn Up The Music\\" for example blends 70's disco/funk and some afro pop percussion into the electro pop framework with a very fully constructed melody. And it's actually a highlight. Again that \\"africaness\\" in the percussion is returned to on the hyperactive closer \\"Trumpet Lights\\". Chris seems to realize the importance of percussion and African rhythms are to the best quality dance music and he lets it shine on these tracks. But there's more here than that. The production is sleek,rich and uniform from the minimal electronica funk/hip-hop of \\"Bassline\\",the unusually chorded \\"Till I Die\\" and the blunted funky hip-hop of \\"Mirage\\" recorded with Nas. The middle part is particularly impressive. In the beginning Chris is sexually obsessed and profane to the point of obnoxiousness. By the middle,on the pulsing,sleek ballads of \\"Don't Judge Me\\",\\"Biggest Fan\\",\\"Sweet Love\\" and in particular the almost Sade like \\"2012\\" he's dealing with his sensitive side,showcasing the male side of that emotional construct and that it is possible.<br /><br />  \\"Strip\\" is a terrific uptempo number blending some modern electro effects with what's basically \\"old school\\" 70's dance/funk. On \\"Stuck On Stupid\\" and \\"4 Years Old\\" Chris is working his way around almost epic folk type melodies with some wonderful lyrics exploring the coming of age process that comes with the realization of male vulnerability. Considering his recent history that's a very good thing indeed. The medley of \\"Party Hard/Cadillac\\" features a near acapella doo-wop style closer with powerful female singer Sevyn. Only \\"Dont Wake Up\\" evokes anything close to throwaway electropop. But even that's so fully realized as a composition I totally forgive that. Overall this album finds Chris Brown taking a particular style of dance music that's today often associated with just about everything that's flawed and disposable  about dance music and applies rhythmic and melodic complication with them. That gives the music the effect of being modern enough to dancers looking only for beats,but also musically rich enough for casual listening as well. It takes from the past,remains in the present and looks to the future. If Chris chooses to expand his music from this level,even better things are likely to come.to the point of obnoxiousness. B0060AY85C|4.0|\\"Home\\" was a surprise for me because it is so fundamentally different from most of Morrison's previous works.  However the simplicity and beauty of this shockingly brief novel is refreshing.  It reads much like a short story in a sense, without the density and complexity of say \\"Beloved\\" or \\"Jazz\\".  I finished this book in a few hours, however it left me with a feeling of satisfaction and warmth.  Its story is one that may resonate with many Americans, particularly those with Southern roots or those who have experienced the aftermath of war veterans or heroes attempting to make an adjustment coming home from Korea or similarly any previous war.  I love the intimacy and grittiness of the characters as they are introduced to the reader.  We find out in a few passages why Cee feels so vulnerable and is an easy target for a doctor who has few ethics and whose scientific experiments take precedence over the well-being of real human beings.  Cee was scarred emotionally as a child, having been raised by an emotionally-scarred grandmother.  Her hero is her brother Frank, from whom she has to learn how to reach her own inner strength so that she might eventually embrace her own life and worth.  Again, I love this book as it touches my heart and inspires me to re-create images from my own family history,  even though on a literary level, critics will most likely not give this work the stature of many of Toni Morrison's previous ones.  Bravo to Toni for a real, heart-warming story.

055338466X|3.0|Stephen Hawking's attempt to answer the great questions about life, the universe and everything. It is co written with someone with a name I can neither pronounce nor spell, but apologies to Leonard Ml-whatever-your-name-is, for not taking the time to copy and paste it.<br /><br />Plusses are that this book is a wonderful short history of the growth of scientific thought as well as a crash course in quantum mechanics, relativity and M theory.<br /><br />However, there was nothing actually new here - although it brings a lot of material into one nicely accessible place. A fuller discussion of the scientific theories can be found in books such as Brian Greene's The Elegant Universe (he has a later book that probably covers any recent changes Hawking includes).<br /><br />As for the history of the development of science, that can be found in many places, but at times it was reminiscent of Russell's History of Western Philosophy.<br /><br />On the downside, this book does not do what it purports to do. It asks the question \\"why is there something rather than nothing\\", but cannot answer it, and the attempt to reduce it to a non question is just a fudge that admits to the unanswerability of the question.<br /><br />Early on the book also simply dismisses philosophy, saying it has not kept up with the science. That statement is a bold assertion written in defiance of the clear fact that modern philosophers are well aware of the latest physics, and make good use of it. Indeed, inasmuch as this book IS a work of philosophy, the book refutes its own assertion.<br /><br />In a few other places, things are asserted without evidence and which are not obviously true.<br /><br />The proposal of the unmodified radical multiverse idea as a solution to the extremely unlikely balance of the laws of nature that allow our universe to exist is also written in ignorance of the work of philosophers such as Keith Ward, who make the point that this merely multiplies the improbability of thewhole. There is now so much more *stuff* to explain than before. We have mechanism but no answer to the reason there is something at all.<br /><br />So no answers to the big questions in this book. I liked the physics, which is nicely described, but other books do that more thoroughly.

B000002G8H|5.0|As I get older, I find that I tend to prefer well-chosen compilations to original albums. Clapton here produces a great selection of songs, melodic, rocking, brilliantly arranged and, as you'd expect, wonderfully performed. From the hard rock of 'The Core' through the bright shuffle of 'Lay Down Sally' to the exquisite tunefulness of 'Wonderful Tonight', there isn't a dud on the album. Every song is like a familiar friend and I never get tired of meeting them again. If every album were like this, the musical world would be a better place; and, incidentally, there would be fewer compilation albums, because there's nothing I would discard from this recording.

They collect immense swaths of history from around the world to support their theory of why nations fail.<br /><br />In the same fashion as Jared Diamond's Guns, Germs and Steel, an excellent book in its own right, they compare contemporaneous societies to better explain why some succeed and others fail.  Their theory is that states that have extractive (or better said exclusive) political participation generate extractive economies which over time obstruct Schumpter's creative destruction from working and thus, these countries fall farther and farther behind the world.  In contrast, pluralistic societies engender more pluralistic (or perhaps distributive) economies which allow creative destruction to work properly and over time, innovation takes off.<br /><br />To prove their point, they go over Roman history, talk a great deal as to why the industrial revolution emerged in Great Britain and discuss the failure of various states from Spanish Latin America to rise of political plurality in early Virginia outposts.  Probably their most interesting example was how Venice first emerged as an innovative powerhouse and then over time fell back by eliminating pluralism in their economy and ultimately in their political establishment.  But they have many more, such as the economic powerhouse that is Botswana as compared to the failed nation state of Zimbabwe.<br /><br />They believe that Communist China in its present form is a more exclusive or extractive political environment and has thereby created an extractive economy.  Over time they believe it's innovation will run out of steam, unless it can transform itself to a more pluralistic political society.<br /><br />Essentially, pluralism means no one group can impede the evolution of technology. Schumpters creative destruction says that for innovation to take place, new technologies will necessarily push aside older ways of doing business and as some ways of doing business are destroyed, somebody in power gets hurt.  But with more pluralistic political establishments that someone cannot hold back the tide for long.<br /><br />However, within an exclusive political environment, those in power can hold back innovation and stop the pain for a long time. For example, in much of Ottoman Empire (Turkey, Arabia, etc.) there were no printing presses until the mid-1800s, almost 400 years after their introduction in Germany.  Why, because the religious and political orders felt that the ability to widely disseminate ideas, even biblical texts would make the religious hierarchy less authoritative and their political power less stable.  (Witness Luther's attack against the Catholic church, imagine if the Pope had banned all printing presses throughout Europe or for that matter Twitter and the Arab Spring of today).<br /><br />On the downside the book needs some serious editing.  It seemed to me that I was reading, multiple times how the Kongo deteriorated, how Latin America succumbed to extractive policies and how Great Britain emerged as a more pluralistic society.  That and their repetitive use of language, got boring.<br /><br />Nevertheless, as I got further into the book it was hard to put down.  Their logic is hard to deny and I am VERY impressed by their command of history around the globe from Roman times to modern day Africa. They certainly have an impressive theory and they amass lots of material to justify it. I just wish they could have done a better job at conveying it.

B007UQ5Z1G|2.0|Linkin Park fans tend to divide into two factions, before and after Minutes to Midnight. As a Rolling Stone poll of the best LP songs just showed this week, the majority are in the pre-minutes corner with just one song out of 20 comming from 1000 suns and no songs from Minutes.<br /><br />Living things comes from the same fire as 1000 suns, but minus the message, the artifact songs and the interwoven speeches. The response to Living Things is much as it was to \\"The Catalyst\\" and that is \\"where does the house music actually fit in here?\\" It's not the lack of power that brings this record down, it's the addition of fluff. Seriously, take away the lyrics and you have a soundtrack for Magic Mike.<br /><br />From the opening synth we know the piping, up-tempo, thumping club music is not only a radical departure for LP but a poor contrast and juxtaposition to Chester's lyrics and style. On A Thousand Suns everything felt like a tripy, techno experiment but on the more straight-forward Living Things it sounds like an awful misstep. Mike feels in need of constant reassurance here<br /><br />\\"You lost that right / to hold that crown<br />I built you up but you let me down\\"<br />\\"I don't hold back I hold my own<br />I can't be mapped / I can't be cloned<br />I can't C-flat / it ain't my tone\\"<br /><br />Maybe he needs to forgo use of the word \\"I\\" because on this and 1000 Suns he keeps telling use how he really is cool, no matter what we say. While Chester's earnest \\"Each word gets lost in the echo' feels all the more relevant surrounded by Mikes unending self-affirmations. He's the Stuart Smally of rock-rap.<br /><br />By the time \\"I'll be gone\\" comes around you may not have noticed the songs changing, they feel so similar you could just fade them into each other. Then, suddenly this techno version of ... wait for it ... The Carpenters (go on kids, You-Tube that!) comes along and we're on a folksy, techno trip to the outer limits.<br /><br />Not that it can't get worse. \\"Victimized\\" tries to reaffirm the bands heavy roots much, actually exactly, the way St. Anger did for Metallica. A big, hollow drum noise with raw vocals, but first a techno-rap verse, now, scream \\"NEVER AGAIN, VICTIMIZED!\\" is a mess.<br /><br />Unlike 1000 Suns, which at least begged you to keep listening (as Chester wanted it to be one long uncut song and released an mp3 version of it that way) Living Things feels like a forced attempt to return to the rap-scream formula of Hybrid Theory and Meteora, but adding the pounding club techno in as if to say it's still something new. Well, new and better aren't the same thing, just different would have been fine, but an utter and complete misfire is what we have here.<br /><br />Living things is as light as air and as flimsy as plastic. The ever moaning \\"look, I am too cool\\" Rap with the \\"feel my pain\\" rock wears thin in one song, let alone 12. Worse yet, is you can take the band away and not notice. This entire record could be performed live with just Chester, Mike and Hahn and no one would notice. LP is not progressing as a band, they are struggling to find a voice, but, they already had one, they just needed to embrace it and trust in themselves to no re-write the first two albums rather than force themselves to do something completely misguided.imized\\" tries to reaffirm the bands heavy roots much, actually exactly, the way St. Anger did for Metallica. 0307592731|3.0|Strayed's memoir of her life and her on and off hike on the PCT are interesting enough, but not in the same context as [[ASIN:0060730552 Touching the Void: The True Story of One Man's Miraculous Survival]] or [[ASIN:B004VXKTT4 STACY'S STORY]].  I expected this to be a suspenseful read, showcasing the protagonist's personal holocaust and what she went through to survive it.  In the two aforementioned books the protagonists do not contrive to place themselves in jeopardy nor do they seek to place blame or question their misfortune; they are simply too busy trying to remain alive.  Their next step does not result in a stubbed toe or broken fingernail, but places them inches closer to exiting through death's door, a component crucial to a great thriller.  In Wild, one gets the impression the protagonist goes out of her way to throw her life over a cliff, repeatedly, (Infidelity, drugs, irreverence, heedless sex) and then laments the impact of the back-draft.  In the book, she's not vulnerable to the loss of life or limb, except through ill-advised self-infliction.  Strayed's antagonist, as she so mindfully reveals in her memoir, is herself, exacerbated by poor choices.  If you're looking for a story on not what to do after you've been jostled by life, then this may be it.  If you're looking for a good suspenseful thriller or inspirational story, try elsewhere.

For me, it was \\"you've got be kidding\\".  I stayed hopeful that it might round this annoying corner and still end with a bang, or something that felt satisfying and true to the characters, but it did not.<br /><br />So, three stars for an engrossing read and well-drawn characters, minus two stars for the awful ending.

It's not a separate browser with an icon, but from the LastPass app you can connect and browse.  Unlike other mobile devices, this app does not seem to work with other apps installed on the Fire.  On those devices, you can select a different input method and then LastPass becomes a \\"keyboard\\" where you can use the AutoFill functionality to login.  However, when browsing the web using the built in browser, the LastPass autofill functionality does not work - unless you install the Dolphin browser and the LastPass plugin.  Only then does the functionality mimic the experience used on the Mac/PC.<br /><br />So, if you only have a Kindle and no other mobile devices, then you may not want to shell out the $12 a year for this app.  However, if you have other devices (blackberry, android mobile, iphone) and are looking for the one app to help you manage all your passwords - the $12 covers all your mobile devices not just the Kindle. 1400095204|5.0|Half of a Yellow Sun takes place in Nigeria during the Nigeria/Biafa civil war. The narrative follows 3 characters: Ugwu, a village boy who is taken in by some politically-inclined academics as a house boy; Olanna, Ugwu's mistress and a rich heiress; and Richard, a British expat who desperately wants to be accepted by the Biafrans as one of them. The stories of these three characters are superbly and tragically woven together on a backdrop of war, racial hatred, and famine. This is one of the most impressive books I've read in quite a while. The characters were so deep that I felt I knew them. The events described had an eerie realism to them that comes from the author's intimate knowledge of the history and people. This is one of those books that makes you feel like every incident described is important and well-planned. 0812993292|3.0|Normally, I really enjoy small, slice-of-life stories about ordinary people, which is why I ordered the Unlikely Pilgrimage of Harold Fry. It has been compared to [[ASIN:0812981227 Major Pettigrew's Last Stand: A Novel]], and in fact, the cover includes a glowing testimonial by that book's author. But while I absolutely loved \\"Major Pettigrew,\\" I was less enamored of \\"Harold Fry.\\" I realize, based on the other reviews, that makes me something of a contrarian, but although the concept was good (though not groundbreaking--David Grossman's To the End of the Land had a similar premise), by its very nature the book was episodic and a bit slow, kind of plodding along like Harold himself. The author did her best to get inside the heads of Harold and his wife, Maureen, and she introduced the requisite colorful characters along the way. She also hinted at elements of the back story that weren't revealed fully until the end. And the main characters were changed, as they need to be in all good fiction.    But the book and its characters never really engaged me the way Major Pettigrew's cast did. Just as there was a sameness to many of Harold's days (at least until he was joined by a group of hangers-on), there was a predictability and repetitiveness to many of the chapters--lots of description of the weather, the countryside and the roads, physical challenges and various encounters with mostly kind people. And toward the end, some of the events were just implausible. It was simply hard to believe, for example, that some of the characters would pour their hearts out to complete strangers in the way they did. It was as if the author was using the device for convenience, to reveal parts of the story, without much regard for whether it was believable. Even one of the pivotal back story events, which I won't reveal for fear of spoiling, didn't make sense. Why would the character, a number cruncher, be cleaning someone else's office?    Rachel Joyce clearly has great potential as awriter--she knows how to describe the world she's depicting and is insightful about her characters--but this book, her first, didn't really resonate with me, primarily because of the pacing and the implausibility of some of the elements. Perhaps her next one will.

B007Y1JGOY|4.0|This one was a long time coming. In fact, this album was originally slated for release in March of 2012, but issues with band's label forced the album into questionable territory. Long story short: Delain's label was recently purchased by Warner Bros.' music division. Since the new label doesn't really \\"get\\" metal, Delain's new album was put on hold until the execs could figure out what they wanted to do with the band. After dealing with (I'm sure) torrents of political crap, Delain announced earlier this year that they had moved to a new label, and their new album, We Are the Others, would be released, finally, in June 2012.<br /><br />And it was well worth the wait for Delain fans.<br /><br />We Are the Others, along with the title song, is inspired by the 2007 hate crime against Sophie Lancaster, a young British girl and identified member of the goth subculture. Sophie and her boyfriend were brutally attacked in a park in Lancashire, UK by a group of young men. After being rushed to the hospital in critical condition, Sophie's boyfriend survived. Sophie did not. It was later determined that Sophie was attacked, and ultimately murdered, purely because of her unique appearance. We Are the Others stands as a memorial to Sophie and as a call for acceptance for those of any unique \\"subculture\\" or anyone who is just a little different from the norm.<br /><br />Seemingly built on the central message of acceptance, many of the songs on this album revolve around those ideas, such as \\"Mother Machine,\\" \\"Hit Me With Your Best Shot,\\"<br /><br />\\"Not Enough\\" and title song \\"We Are the Others.\\" For me, the real stand out song on this album is \\"We Are the Others,\\" a powerful anthem for anyone who doesn't fit the with \\"in\\" crowd that celebrates acceptance. \\"Mother Machine\\" is an unique track with heavy guitars and a gorgeous string bridge. Though it came across as somewhat sinister, I enjoyed the heavier sound and the industrial/mechanical influences. \\"Not Enough,\\" though a little on the lighter side, probably brings home the album's main themes the best, while \\"Hit Me With Your Best Shot,\\" in its surprisingly snyth-y glory, encourages those who have been bullied or hurt to be strong.<br /><br />I was a little surprised at the lyrical inconsistency when it came to themes. Since this album was so strongly grounded in such a horrible tragedy (and such a strong central message), I expected virtually the entire album to flow from that, but that's not what happened. That's an almost odd variety of themes here, ranging from acceptance to lost love, romantic obsession and romantic conflict. I really would have liked to have seen the album centered more on the acceptance themes, it would have added some cohesion and made the overall album stronger.<br /><br />But that doesn't mean that the album was a bust past those four song. In fact, far from it. We Are the Others offers some haunting songs wrapped with catchy hooks, a shocking amount of snyth, the occasional orchestral section, heavier guitars and the most amazing vocals lead singer Charlotte Wessels has ever delivered. For me, highlights included: \\"Generation Me,\\" \\"Get the Devil Out of Me,\\" and \\"Are You Done With Me?\\" \\"Generation Me\\" offers some interesting commentary on social media, modern Internet narcissism and the concept of an online \\"persona while the super-catchy \\"Get the Devil Out of Me\\" takes Delain's signature lighter approach to symphonic rock with a bit of that rock edge. \\"Are You Done With Me?\\" actually threw me off at first with its super poppy and radio-friendly feel, but it grew on me after a while. I blame it on the incredible vocal work. Charlotte jumps effortlessly between notes and between genres on the chorus, and it's both haunting and beautiful.<br /><br />Overall, We Are the Others completely \\"fixed\\" all of the issues I had with April Rain. The songs are more polished, they don't feel over thought or overwrought and the overall direction is clearer. While the band didstick to the lighter side of the genre, the guitars were heavier than on April Rain, there seemed to be more snyth and less use of the orchestral aspect of the genre (and there's even a childrens' choir on \\"We Are the Others\\"). And Charlotte is incredible here. Her voice shines on every track with its brilliant range and amazing power -truly her best performance thus far.<br /><br />On the whole, We Are the Others is a much stronger album than April Rain. Yet, there is still room for improvement. Some of the songs started to run together, and I personally would like to see a more expanded use of the orchestra and greater thematic consistency, but that's really a personal preference.<br /><br />Delain's latest album is worth the wait for fans, and I'm excited to see what this band does in the future.

B005XBA9Y8|4.0|I have received this set from Amazon and have listened with particular interest in trying to figure out what makes these recordings different.  The set contains many performances -- some great, none less than good -- from core repertoire.  Then there's the other \\"stuff\\" that is less interesting to me -- Civil War (2 discs), Flamenco guitar, and a lot of Band stuff from Fennell.  But my main interest is \\"Why does the orchestra sound different in these recordings than others contemporary with the times?\\" (i.e. early stereo era -- 1960's)<br /><br />I believe I have an idea how this Living Presence came to be.  The Mercury Team used three microphones -- so did RCA.  But the difference seems to be that Mercury used the middle mike for stereo purposes whereas RCA's engineers referred to that mike as the \\"mono mike\\" (from which they presumably mastered the parallel mono version of the recording).  Many years ago I had lunch with Dr. Harvey Fletcher -- generally credited with both bringing sound to the world of physics AND the invention of stereophonic sound.  He told us (six of us around a lunch table) that they had shown empirically (back in the '30's) that the stereo effect was best achieved with three channels.  He insisted that two channels was not stereo but binaural.<br /><br />I believe what the Mercury team did was to take more care with that center channel and thus produced recordings that were so carefully balanced with that channel that they were able to produce a more accurate virtual center channel for our standard two channel world.  By doing so, they brought a greater focus to the center of the orchestra where the woodwinds live.  The term \\"presence\\" in the sound world has generally meant more mid-range.  Some early stereo equipment had standard treble and bass controls but then had a \\"presence\\" control between them which was intended to control the mid frequencies.  By more carefully recording the center channel there really is more presence than we're used to because the center of the orchestra comes into clearer focus and does not wander around the sound stage.<br /><br />Thus these Mercury (orchestral) recordings have a more natural stereo spread, as well.  It is not a case where the violins are crammed into the left speaker while the trumpets and basses are crammed into the right.  If the concertmaster plays a solo within the piece, he comes from just right of center -- right where the sound should originate.  This is not the case with many recordings.  Mercury got it right.  The middle mike was not treated as \\"the mono mike\\" but rather as the center channel -- exactly like Dr. Fletcher described to us around that lunch table years ago.<br /><br />So the bottom line is that these recordings ARE special because they (1) focus the center of the orchestra and by doing so (2) they increase the \\"warmth\\" (i.e. mid-range) and create a much more intimate sound -- more like the conductor would hear on the podium.  I didn't buy many of them back in the '60's but I am very pleased to now have them and know what I was missing.  Highly recommended.<br /><br />PS:  4 stars only because there are too many novelty discs in this set that have nothing to do with the core classical repertoire.<br /><br />PPS:  Ironically, the \\"Living Presence\\" moniker actually was born during the late mono era.  But I think it describes their stereo results perfectly.d to because the center of the orchestra comes into clearer focus and does not wander around the sound stage.<br /><br />Thus these Mercury (orchestral) recordings have a more natural stereo spread, as well.  It is not a case where the violins are crammed into the left speaker while the trumpets and basses are crammed into the right.  If the concertmaster plays a solo within the piece, he comes from just right of center -- right where the sound should originate.  This is not the case with many recordings.  Mercury got it right. But I think it describes their stereo results perfectly.

B001CZVVW8|4.0|This is a very fine performance and recording of what must be described as rather a minimalist production which, on its own terms, must also be considered a success. To elaborate further therefore ......<br /><br />The cast chosen for this production is unquestioningly top quality throughout and it delivers similar high quality performances in depth. The title role of Onegin perfectly suits the handsome and impressive Dmitri Hvorostovsky whose rich voice and generally restrained bearing underlines Onegin's own perceived role in society. The Russian timbre of his voice also adds considerable veracity to this role. In this respect he is in good company within the cast. Olga, sung by Elena Zaremba, Tatiana's nurse, sung by Larisa Shevchenko, Tatiana's mother sung by Svetlana Volkova and the impressively Russian timbred Serei Aleksashkin as Prince Gremin, all share these tonal advantages. All the above listed singers are clearly equally immersed in their respective roles and the production benefits accordingly.<br /><br />Ren&eacute;e Fleming is a perfect Tatiana and brings the necessary appearance of youth combined with her later social sophistication effortlessly to the role. Her voice is as beautiful as her acting is beyond reproach. The role of Lenski suits the sustained lyrical abilities of Ram&oacute;n Vargas which culminates in arguably one of his finest moments at the beginning of Act 2 scene 2 in his lengthy aria just prior to the fatal duel. This justifiably earns a considerable ovation from the very appreciative audience.<br /><br />The orchestra play to their normal high standard under the sure direction of Gergiev who clearly knows this score inside out.<br /><br />The production is one of the minimalist variety where the intention appears to be to focus the audience's attention on the personal dramas rather than the settings. To this extent it succeeds very well and suits the intention of the libretto based largely on Pushkin's creation. As Tchaikovsky himself commented in a letter to his future benefactress Nadezhda von Meck on 27 May 1877, `The opera will have no strong dramatic action, but the portrayal of everyday life will be interesting, and how full of poetry it all is.' Nevertheless there will be those who will regret the lack of opulence in the ballroom scenes which can be spectacular in other productions.<br /><br />There are the usual interviews and introductions typical of these Met recordings plus a short film giving an interesting glimpse of rehearsal moments which reinforce expectations rather than casting any new light as regards understanding this opera. The booklet is usefully informative.<br /><br />The imaging is crisp and the camera work is involving. The close-up shots during the ballroom scenes help to suggest a greater scale of event than is actually the case on stage! The sound is presented in excellent DTS 5.1 and stereo.<br /><br />This is a quality production which should give plenty of pleasure and satisfaction to most purchasers. Within the context of the somewhat minimalist chosen production values it seems reasonable to rate this as a convincing disc although there will be many who will regret the lack of scenic scale, most obviously at points such as the ballroom scene where it can easily be argued that too much has to be left to the imagination.<br /><br />...........................................<br /><br />A response to a previous voter of this review:<br /><br />Goodness knows what you find to be unhelpful about this review<br /><br />The voting system is specifically only about reviews being 'helpful' or 'unhelpful'<br />A negative vote without reason is not helpful to anyone. B004DIPL3Q|5.0|Lorin Maazel considered requests to create a synthesis of Wagner's Ring cycle as a continuous `Ring without Words' twice before finally agreeing to the request made by the recording company, Telarc, in 1987. The resultant 75 minute recording proved to be a big seller and in Maazel's opinion it helped to create a new audience for the operas.<br /><br />In creating this continuous synthesis Maazel attempted to produce a reduced version which followed strict chronological order and which introduced all the main themes and motifs without adding a single note not written by Wagner. In this he reinforced Wieland Wagner's view (Wagner's Grandson) that the essence of the work lies in the orchestral score.<br /><br />Weiland Wagner's view is worth considering in this context and can be quoted as stated to Maazel at a rehearsal of the Ring as `The orchestra, that's where it all is - the text behind the text, the universal subconscious that binds Wagner's personae one to another and to the proto-ego of legend ....'<br /><br />As one who has known The Ring cycle for around 40 years or so this synthesis is both impressive and intriguing. It is also successful in terms of what was attempted as in paragraphs 1-2 above. It is impressive because it covers so many of the key moments and intriguing because it jumps unexpectedly from one key point to another without including what you would expect to hear next! It becomes a sort of quiz - spot the excerpt, and a rather enjoyable one too!<br /><br />The whole experience is enormously enhanced by the wonderful playing of the Berlin Philharmonic at full strength. Four harps is an impressive sight not to mention vast ranks of strings and incisively burnished brass. If ever an orchestra was born to play this music it has to be this one. The visual impact provides a real advantage over audio only reproduction.<br /><br />In terms of reproduction, this early example of high definition recording from 2000 is a complete success with crisp imaging of considerable clarity and fully involving camera work. The sound is wide ranging and presented in DTS-HD 5.1 as well as stereo. There is a short bonus in which Maazel says much of what is relayed above and which is even more fully covered in the sleeve notes.<br /><br />As a keen Wagner Ring enthusiast, I still found this a very enjoyable experience for all the reasons above. I would expect it to give much pleasure to all but the most hardened of purists and, in addition, it might be a very useful way of getting to know the basics of the opera cycle as an introduction in miniature so to speak. For all the above reasons therefore, and in my own personal opinion, it seems only reasonable to rate this as a 5 star issue.ng of considerable clarity and fully involving camera work. For all the above reasons therefore, and in my own personal opinion, it seems only reasonable to rate this as a 5 star issue.
