0307887448|5.0|I confess: I steered clear of this book for a long time because the title made me think that its subject matter - i.e. gaming - would be something in which I am not well-versed.  Then, something interesting happened - I started seeing lots of different people pick up a copy, and it seemed to transcend all of the usual boundaries, in that both men and women and everyone from teens to grandparents seemed to be gravitating toward it.  This all culminated in a woman coming up to me and telling me how her daughter had urged her to read it.  She couldn't stop raving about it, so I finally picked up a copy.  And now I can definitely see what all the fuss was about.<br /><br />The most important thing for prospective readers to know is that you don't have to be a gamer to enjoy this.  It's true that gaming receives a lot of focus, but it's all explained in such a matter-of-fact way that nothing goes over your head, nor does the material become too bogged down in technical jargon.<br /><br />Since there have already been so many reviews about the book's plot, I'll mostly stick to more general impressions about the content and characters.  In regard to the latter, Wade is a great main character: he is a normal, 18-year-old boy who happens to be absolutely brilliant when it comes to gaming and seemingly all things technology-related.  He's witty and a little angsty; he makes mistakes, but he also makes grand, heroic gestures, and everything is backed up with a staggering amount of knowledge and smarts.  His relationships and friendships with the supporting players all begin and/or develop naturally, and I think Mr. Cline did a wonderful job of keeping his characterizations grounded in reality even as their lives were immersed almost entirely in virtual reality.  When it comes to the big bad of the piece, he could have been a cartoony, mustache-twirling, cardboard cutout kind of villain, but he was actually quite cold and menacing, which is made all the more potent because he,too, is operating with a lot of intelligence.<br /><br />As great as they are, though, the characters are not the star of the show, the virtual reality is.  It was described in such expansive, even jaw-dropping detail; considering we're talking about innumerable planets, structures, puzzles, combat scenarios, and the like, it could easily have gotten bogged down in the details.  I see that some other reviewers found that to be the case, but for me, it all served the purpose of bringing each of these aspects of the virtual reality game to life, and I think Cline succeeded brilliantly at that world-building, too.<br /><br />On top of that, the reader gets treated to myriad references to all things from the 1980's - so many TV shows, movies, songs, and of course, games are mentioned and it becomes so much nostalgic fun to read about all of it.  Granted, I was born in the early part of that decade, so some of the references weren't entirely familiar to me, but then he would mention things like Family Ties, the Commodore 64, Max Headroom, Bladerunner and Indiana Jones, Ferris Bueller and WarGames, \\"Wake Me Up Before You Go Go,\\" and Zork (my older sister was *obsessed* with those games), and I was transported right back to my childhood.<br /><br />Plus, he referenced the Firefly universe being created adjacent to those of Star Wars and Star Trek.  After reading that near the beginning of the book, I was hooked, courtesy of my deep and abiding love of Firefly.  Cline paid homage to umpteen pop culture references, both from the past and even some from present-day, and he did so with great inventiveness and wit.<br /><br />Though the book may be a lot of fun, that is not to say that it doesn't include elements that are dark and/or highly emotional, like murder, corporate greed and corruption, first love and first heartbreak, and even an epic battle.  It also contains a sort of cautionary message about escapism, i.e. how a little can be a good thing, and can lead to inspiration and creativity, but how, like any number of other subjects and pastimes, too much of a good thing can start to be harmful when it results in people willfully abandoning reality in ever-increasing amounts.  The book tackled this thought head-on and literally, but not in a ham-fisted way, which held true for all of those other less light-hearted themes, as well.<br /><br />In short (which is ironic, given the length of this review), then, Cline was able to deftly juggle both fun and heavy subjects and situations, and in doing so, he created one of the most purely entertaining, wholly creative books that I've had the pleasure of reading in a long time.  I would urge people who might find themselves hesitant to pick this one up (like I was) to give it a try - you'll be glad you did.

0307594165|1.0|[CAUTION -- MAY CONTAIN SPOILERS!]<br /><br />After loving \\"The Bluest Eye\\", \\"Song of Solomon\\", and \\"Beloved\\", Toni Morrison's \\"Home\\" was a colossal let down. But that was just completely absent here.<br /><br />The story is about a black Korean war veteran, who was said to come home and experience racism, and his sister, who was described as being \\"medically abused\\" and in need of rescue. Already that sounded like a fabulous plot, especially in the hands of a highly intelligent and gifted writer like Morrison. This also takes into account the moving poem that started the book off, which hinted to blacks being foreigners in the land of their birth because of racism: \\"...why does this lock fit my [house]key?\\"<br /><br />But after I finished the book, I wondered what was even her point of writing it: there was no racism experienced by the main character, not even subtly--what was his conflict? Ultimately he seemed like a caricature of what we all believe a war-torn veteran is. And though his sister was a victim of a eugenics doctor, even that victimization had no point to it--one would expect Toni Morrison to sensitively and powerfully delve into this part of America's racial history. She then brought up, almost sloppily and as an after-thought, this idea that whites had a fighting club where they forced blacks to fight each other to the death. For anyone who watched the movie \\"Mandingo\\", these \\"fighting blacks\\" were also featured, fighting in a gruesome and savage display for their slave-holders. Again, Morrison could have also crafted something powerful about this historical fact, especially since she implied that father and son were inhumanely pitted against one another. But nothing further was even said about the subject.<br /><br />Perhaps \\"Home\\" suffered from extreme brevity when considering the fact itdealt with numerous heavy topics (post-traumatic stress, eugenics abuses, black objectification). I believe if Morrison would have added about 150 to 200 pages to the book, it would have been a much better work.<br /><br />Additionally, the book also suffered from bad prose. The other books from Morrison that I'd read were beautifully written: she wrote incredible opening sentences to every chapter that had a way of hooking you right in; she had a keen, wise realistic eye... her works were simply magic. This book seemed like the choppy, dull prose that characterizes so much of today's literary fiction--it's just downright yucky. I was hoping to read 1970s-1980s Toni Morrison, but she wasn't there.<br /><br />\\"Home\\" was a failure--so many missed opportunities! Not recommended. Re-read \\"The Bluest Eye\\" or \\"Sula\\" if you want novella-length, high quality, vintage Toni Morrison.
